###begin article-title 0
###xml 48 51 48 51 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 138 154 138 154 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 138 154 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
Intragenic suppressors of temperature-sensitive rne mutations lead to the dissociation of RNase E activity on mRNA and tRNA substrates in Escherichia coli
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
Present addresses: Danyal Hami, University Program in Genetics and Genomics, Duke University, Durham, NC 27710, USA Zhao Min, Department of Cancer Biology, Scripps Florida, Jupiter, FL 33458, USA
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 0 16 0 16 <!--CREATIVE COMMONS-->

###end p 2
###begin p 3
###xml 11 27 11 27 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 170 175 170 175 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 180 188 180 188 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-3071</italic>
###xml 502 507 502 507 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 512 520 512 520 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-3071</italic>
###xml 589 596 589 596 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 786 806 786 806 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/172, rne-1/186</italic>
###xml 811 820 811 820 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/187</italic>
###xml 917 922 917 922 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 11 27 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
RNase E of Escherichia coli is an essential endoribonuclease that is involved in many aspects of RNA metabolism. Point mutations in the S1 RNA-binding domain of RNase E (rne-1 and rne-3071) lead to temperature-sensitive growth along with defects in 5S rRNA processing, mRNA decay and tRNA maturation. However, it is not clear whether RNase E acts similarly on all kinds of RNA substrates. Here we report the isolation and characterization of three independent intragenic second-site suppressors of the rne-1 and rne-3071 alleles that demonstrate for the first time the dissociation of the in vivo activity of RNase E on mRNA versus tRNA and rRNA substrates. Specifically, tRNA maturation and 9S rRNA processing were restored to wild-type levels in each of the three suppressor mutants (rne-1/172, rne-1/186 and rne-1/187), while mRNA decay and autoregulation of RNase E protein levels remained as defective as in the rne-1 single mutant. Each single amino acid substitution (Gly-->Ala at amino acid 172; Phe --> Cys at amino acid 186 and Arg --> Leu at amino acid 187) mapped within the 5' sensor region of the RNase E protein. Molecular models of RNase E suggest how suppression may occur.
###end p 3

INTRODUCTION

###xml 82 98 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
Ribonuclease E (RNase E, encoded by the rne gene) is an essential endonuclease in Escherichia coli that plays a major role in many aspects of RNA metabolism (1,2). It is involved in the decay of a large number of mRNAs (3-5), the processing of 5S rRNA (3,6) and the maturation of many tRNA precursors (7,8). RNase E, a 1061 aa protein, is comprised of two distinct regions of defined function: a highly conserved amino-terminal portion of the polypeptide that contains the substrate-binding and catalytic domains (9); and a non-catalytic, largely unstructured, less conserved carboxy-terminal region that also acts as a scaffold for a multienzyme complex called the degradosome (10) (Figure 1). The degradosome includes the 3'-->5' exonuclease polynucleotide phosphorylase (PNPase), the ATP-dependent DEAD-box RNA helicase (RhlB) and the glycolytic enzyme enolase (11-14). Figure 1.Diagrammatic representation of RNase E protein. The upper portion of the figure indicates the two major regions of the protein: the N-terminal catalytic domain; and the C-terminal degradosome scaffolding region. The C-terminal region, composed of an arginine-rich binding domain (RBD) (10), a second arginine-rich noncatalytic region (AR2) that binds RNA independent of the longer RBD (16) and the binding regions of polynucleotide phosphorylase (PNPase), RNA helicase (RhlB) and enolase, is based on the work of Vanzo et al. (10). The C-terminal region is drawn after Chandran et al. (67). The lower portion of the figure is an expanded view of the N-terminal catalytic domain showing the five subdomains identified from the X-ray crystallographic analysis of Callaghan et al. (23). The locations of the various rne mutations are indicated. rne-1 (Gly to Ser); rne-3071 (Leu to Phe); rne-172 (Gly to Ala); rne-186 (Phe to Cys) and rne-187 (Arg to Leu). The figure is not drawn to scale.
###end p 5

###xml 286 288 286 288 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B10">10</xref>
###xml 386 388 386 388 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B16">16</xref>
###xml 519 525 519 525 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al.</italic>
###xml 527 529 527 529 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B10">10</xref>
###xml 578 584 578 584 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al.</italic>
###xml 586 588 586 588 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B67">67</xref>

###xml 862 870 862 870 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-3071</italic>

###end p 6
###begin p 7
###xml 78 83 78 83 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15 B16 B17 B18">15&#8211;18</xref>
###xml 134 136 134 136 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
###xml 180 181 180 181 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 182 184 182 184 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 285 286 285 286 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 412 415 412 415 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 428 430 428 430 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 431 433 431 433 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 556 559 556 559 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 629 630 629 630 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 631 633 631 633 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
The degradosome has been implicated in the degradation of untranslated mRNAs (15-18), the sensing of intracellular levels of poly(A) (19), and autoregulation of RNase E synthesis (3,20). However, general mRNA decay is not significantly impaired in the absence of degradosome assembly (3). Autoregulation of RNase E levels is achieved by cleaving specific sequences within the 5' untranslated region (UTR) of the rne transcript (21,22), leading to its rapid decay. Defects in the activity of RNase E impair autoregulation by increasing the stability of the rne transcript and result in higher intracellular levels of the protein (3,21).
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 273 275 273 275 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 403 405 403 405 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B24">24</xref>
###xml 567 569 567 569 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B25">25</xref>
###xml 681 689 681 689 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 691 693 691 693 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
###xml 694 696 694 696 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B27">27</xref>
###xml 789 791 789 791 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
Because of its essential nature, RNase E has been the subject of extensive genetic, biochemical and structural studies aimed at understanding its precise biological role and mechanism of action. According to the three-dimensional crystal structure of the catalytic domain (23), the N-terminal 415-residue polypeptide folds into five distinct regions: (i) the previously described S1 RNA-binding domain (24), (ii) a 5' sensor region, (iii) an RNase H domain, (iv) a DNase I domain and (v) a 'Zn-link' that forms a metal-sharing interface required for oligomerization (25). RNase E binding to the 5'-monophosphate group of an RNA substrate leads to stimulation of catalytic activity in vitro (26,27) and is facilitated by the interaction between the 5' sensor region and the RNase H domain (23).
###end p 8
###begin p 9
###xml 122 124 122 124 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 245 251 245 251 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al.</italic>
###xml 253 255 253 255 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 795 797 795 797 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
Since the catalytic site and the 5' sensing region of RNase E are physically separated as shown by the crystal structure (23), the question arises as to how the status of the 5' end of the substrate is communicated to the active site. Callaghan et al. (23) have proposed a model in which 5' sensing is linked to catalysis through an allosteric change in the protein. According to this induced-fit hypothesis for catalysis, binding to the 5' monophosphate of an RNA substrate rearranges the 5' sensor pocket, which then interacts with the S1 domain causing it to clamp onto the RNA. This structural reorganization orients the phosphate backbone for an in-line attack on the scissile phosphate by a nucleophilic hydroxyl group. Based on the crystal structure and the proposed model for catalysis (23), S1 domain mutants could potentially be rescued by compensatory amino acid changes in the 5' sensor region.
###end p 9
###begin p 10
###xml 159 162 159 162 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 172 177 172 177 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 182 190 182 190 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-3071</italic>
###xml 273 281 273 281 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 284 285 284 285 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B6">6</xref>
###xml 286 288 286 288 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 289 291 289 291 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B29">29</xref>
###xml 517 518 517 518 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 519 520 519 520 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B4">4</xref>
###xml 521 524 521 524 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B6 B7 B8">6&#8211;8</xref>
###xml 525 527 525 527 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B30">30</xref>
###xml 528 530 528 530 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B31">31</xref>
In fact, genetic studies have demonstrated an important role for the S1 domain in the biological activity of RNase E. Specifically, the two well-characterized rne alleles (rne-1 and rne-3071) map within the S1 domain of the protein (at amino acids 66 and 68, respectively, Figure 1) (6,28,29). The phenotypic properties of these mutants include inviability at elevated temperature along with defects in 5S rRNA maturation, tRNA processing, mRNA decay and processing of both tmRNA and the RNA subunit (M1) of RNase P (3,4,6-8,30,31).
###end p 10
###begin p 11
###xml 0 7 0 7 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 74 77 74 77 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng</italic>
###xml 147 149 147 149 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 150 152 150 152 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B33">33</xref>
###xml 221 223 221 223 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B34">34</xref>
###xml 256 263 256 263 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 287 289 287 289 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B35">35</xref>
###xml 290 292 290 292 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 408 413 408 413 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37 B38 B39">37&#8211;39</xref>
###xml 491 492 491 492 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 493 495 493 495 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B35">35</xref>
###xml 496 498 496 498 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B40">40</xref>
###xml 621 626 621 626 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 720 722 720 722 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;8</sup>
###xml 734 736 734 736 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;8</sup>
###xml 738 740 738 740 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 0 7 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
E. coli also encodes a nonessential endoribonuclease, RNase G (product of rng), which is homologous to the N-terminal catalytic domain of RNase E (32,33). While both proteins share a 5'-end-dependent catalytic mechanism (34), RNase G has much more limited in vivo substrate specificity (35,36). It is involved in the maturation of the 5' end of 16S rRNA and cleaves a very limited number of mRNA substrates (37-39). However, it appears to play a very minor role, if any, in tRNA maturation (8,35,40). In fact, it was during experiments designed to determine if RNase G could complement the loss of RNase E activity in an rne-1 mutant that we isolated temperature-resistant revertants at frequencies ranging from 0.3 x 10-8 to 7.0 x 10-8 (36).
###end p 11
###begin p 12
###xml 267 270 267 270 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 384 389 384 389 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 394 402 394 402 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-3071</italic>
###xml 631 636 631 636 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 686 692 686 692 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne131</italic>
###xml 694 696 694 696 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 699 710 699 706 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;610</italic>
###xml 712 713 708 709 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 719 730 715 722 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;645</italic>
###xml 732 733 724 725 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 985 992 977 984 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
While such isolates were previously presumed to be true revertants and had never been systematically studied, here we show that the majority of these temperature-resistant revertants actually arise from intragenic second-site suppressor mutations that map within the rne sequence for the 5' sensor region of RNase E. These suppressors complement the conditional lethality of both the rne-1 and rne-3071 alleles. A unique feature of these intragenic second-site suppressor alleles is that they restore tRNA maturation and 9S rRNA processing to wild-type levels but do not rescue the defect in mRNA decay that is associated with the rne-1 allele. These mutants are thus distinct from the rne131 (20), rneDelta610 (3) and rneDelta645 (8) truncation alleles in which both tRNA maturation and mRNA decay are defective but 5S rRNA maturation is normal. Hence, these findings represent the first reported case in which the activity of RNase E on tRNA and mRNA substrates has been dissociated in vivo. In addition, computer modeling of the altered protein structures suggests a complex interaction between the S1 domain and the 5' sensor region of the protein.
###end p 12

###xml 718 724 700 706 <td xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" rowspan="1" colspan="1">SK2566</td>
###xml 724 730 706 712 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">thyA71</italic>
###xml 732 758 714 740 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 rph-1 recA56 srlD300</italic>
###xml 762 764 744 746 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">10</italic>
###xml 767 768 749 750 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>

###xml 4 11 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 693 696 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
###xml 1089 1092 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
###xml 1359 1362 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
###xml 2975 2978 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
The E. coli strains used in the study (Table 1) were all derivatives of MG1693. pSBK29 (Cmr Aps) was constructed by cleaving pWSK29 (41) with ScaI and inserting a 0.8 kb Cmr cassette derived by cleavage of pKRP10 (42) with HincII. In many cases strains were constructed using the plasmid displacement technique described by Ow et al. (3). ###begin p 16
Bacterial strains and plasmids used in the study
###end p 16
###begin title 17
Growth of bacteria
###end title 17
###begin p 18
All strains were grown in Luria broth supplemented with thymine (50 mug/ml). When needed, antibiotics were added to the cultures at the following concentrations: kanamycin, 25 mug/ml; chloramphenicol, 25 mug/ml; ampicillin, 100 mug/ml and spectinomycin, 25 mug/ml. Growth curves were carried out either at 37degreesC or by shifting to 44degreesC cultures initially grown at 30degreesC, after they had reached approximately 40 Klett units (No. 42 green filter) above background. For growth curves, cultures were maintained in exponential phase by periodic dilutions with fresh pre-warmed medium.
###end p 18
###begin title 19
Isolation and identification of intragenic suppressor alleles
###end title 19
###begin p 20
###xml 16 19 16 19 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 32 33 32 33 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 29 33 29 33 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 45 51 45 51 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/</italic>
###xml 62 63 62 63 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 59 63 59 63 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 75 87 75 87 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 recA56</italic>
###xml 98 99 98 99 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 95 99 95 99 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 114 126 114 126 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 recA56</italic>
###xml 220 221 202 203 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">8</sup>
###xml 581 582 551 552 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 621 622 591 592 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 682 683 652 653 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 679 683 649 653 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 898 903 862 867 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 975 978 939 942 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 1005 1010 969 974 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 1091 1096 1055 1060 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
Strains SK2585 [rne-1/pUGK24(rng+)], SK2593 [rne-1/pUGK31 (rng+)], SK2594 [rne-1 recA56/pUGK31(rng+)] and SK6610 (rne-1 recA56) were grown for 30-50 generations in Luria broth at 37degreesC. Subsequently, approximately108 cells from each culture were spread onto individual Luria agar plates and incubated at 44degreesC for 36 h. Colonies that grew at the nonpermissive temperature were purified and retested by replica plating for their ability to grow at 44degreesC. In the case of SK2585, SK2593 and SK2594, temperature-resistant isolates were transformed either with pSBK29 (Cmr) to displace pUGK24 or with pBR325 (Cmr) to displace pUGK31, the resident plasmids carrying the rng+ gene. The ability of the transformants to grow at 44degreesC in the absence of either pUGK24 or pUGK31 indicated that the potential suppressor mutations were on the chromosome. To rule out the possibility that the rne-1 allele had reverted to wild-type, we initially sequenced the region of rne gene spanning the site of rne-1 mutation from six independent temperature-resistant isolates. In all cases, the rne-1 mutation was retained.
###end p 20
###begin p 21
###xml 25 28 25 28 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 241 243 241 243 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B41">41</xref>
###xml 330 342 330 342 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 recA56</italic>
###xml 658 661 652 655 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
Subsequently, the entire rne-coding sequence along with its promoter region was amplified from eleven independent isolates (four derived from either SK2585, SK2593 or SK2594 and seven obtained from SK6610) using PCR and cloned into pWSK129 (41) as described in the next section. Each new plasmid was then transformed into SK6610 (rne-1 recA56) and the ability of the resulting transformants to grow at 44degreesC was determined by replica plating. In every case, the transformants formed colonies at the non-permissive temperature suggesting that the suppressor mutations were on the cloned DNA segment. To precisely identify the mutations, we amplified the rne gene along with its promoter region from genomic DNA of all eleven independent isolates and subjected them to automated sequencing using an Applied Biosystem 3730xl DNA Analyzer.
###end p 21
###begin title 22
Molecular cloning and site-directed mutagenesis
###end title 22
###begin p 23
###xml 113 116 113 116 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 204 210 204 210 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">GAATTC</bold>
###xml 247 253 247 253 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">TCTAGA</bold>
###xml 334 337 334 337 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Pfu</italic>
###xml 469 470 469 470 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 473 475 473 475 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B41">41</xref>
###xml 607 619 607 615 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 629 630 625 626 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 626 630 622 626 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 633 634 629 630 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 677 678 673 674 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 683 684 679 680 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 687 688 683 684 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">s</sup>
###xml 737 749 733 741 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 764 776 756 764 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 791 803 779 787 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 821 833 805 813 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 880 881 860 861 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 877 881 857 861 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 892 897 872 877 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 911 919 891 899 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-3071</italic>
###xml 981 983 961 963 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B41">41</xref>
###xml 1007 1008 987 988 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1004 1008 984 988 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1019 1024 999 1004 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 1038 1041 1018 1021 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 1100 1112 1080 1088 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 1122 1123 1098 1099 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1119 1123 1095 1099 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1126 1127 1102 1103 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
Genomic DNA from strains SK2580, SK2747, SK2748 and SK9789 was used as templates in PCR reactions to amplify the rne gene along with its promoters. Amplification was done with primers rne1014 (5'-CGGTGCTTGAATTCTCTTCACATGCGACTG) and rne3628 (5'-CATTCTAGATTAGCAAGGATGCCATTC) to introduce EcoRI and XbaI restriction sites (in bold), and Pfu DNA polymerase (Stratagene). Subsequently, the amplified DNA fragments were cleaved with EcoRI and XbaI and cloned into pWSK129 [Kmr, (41)] linearized with the same enzymes. The resulting plasmids (pHK1, pHK5, pHK6 and pHK2, respectively) were transformed into SK9714 [rneDelta1018/pSBK1(rne+ Cmr)] and, by use of plasmid incompatibility (3), Kmr Cms transformants were selected to generate SK9794 (rneDelta1018/pHK1), SK3221(rneDelta1018/pHK5), SK3222(rneDelta1018/pHK6) and SK2742(rneDelta1018/pHK2). Similarly, genomic DNA from MG1693 (rne+), SK6610 (rne-1) and SK7669 (rne-3071) was also amplified by PCR as above and cloned into pWSK129 (41) to produce pUGK19 (rne+), pUGK20 (rne-1) and pUGK46 (rne-3071), respectively. Subsequent transformation of SK9714 [rneDelta1018/pSBK1(rne+ Cmr)] with these plasmids and displacement of pSBK1 produced SK2586, SK3169 and SK3742, respectively.
###end p 23

###xml 1044 1092 1040 1088 <tr xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phk6-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; ATTTGCCGACG<bold>G</bold>CAGCGGTGCGCAC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Gly172Ala</td></tr>
###xml 950 1092 946 1088 <tbody xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" align="left"><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">phk1-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; GTGTTCAGA<bold>A</bold>GGAAGCTTAAATCC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Arg187Leu</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">phk5-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; GTTTCAGACGG<bold>C</bold>AGCTTAAATCCC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phe186Cys</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phk6-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; ATTTGCCGACG<bold>G</bold>CAGCGGTGCGCAC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Gly172Ala</td></tr></tbody>
###xml 919 1092 915 1088 <table xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" frame="hsides" rules="groups"><thead align="left"><tr><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Primer</th><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Sequence</th><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Amino acid change</th></tr></thead><tbody align="left"><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">phk1-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; GTGTTCAGA<bold>A</bold>GGAAGCTTAAATCC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Arg187Leu</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">phk5-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; GTTTCAGACGG<bold>C</bold>AGCTTAAATCCC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phe186Cys</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phk6-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; ATTTGCCGACG<bold>G</bold>CAGCGGTGCGCAC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Gly172Ala</td></tr></tbody></table>
###xml 1092 1152 1088 1148 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="26">The mismatched nt in each mutagenic primer is shown in bold.</p>
###xml 1092 1152 1088 1148 <fn xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="26">The mismatched nt in each mutagenic primer is shown in bold.</p></fn>
###xml 1092 1152 1088 1148 <table-wrap-foot xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><fn><p textid="26">The mismatched nt in each mutagenic primer is shown in bold.</p></fn></table-wrap-foot>
###xml 875 1152 871 1148 <table-wrap xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="T2" position="float"><label>Table 2.</label><caption><p textid="25">Mutagenic primers used in this study</p></caption><table frame="hsides" rules="groups"><thead align="left"><tr><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Primer</th><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Sequence</th><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Amino acid change</th></tr></thead><tbody align="left"><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">phk1-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; GTGTTCAGA<bold>A</bold>GGAAGCTTAAATCC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Arg187Leu</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">phk5-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; GTTTCAGACGG<bold>C</bold>AGCTTAAATCCC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phe186Cys</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phk6-mut</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">5&#8242; ATTTGCCGACG<bold>G</bold>CAGCGGTGCGCAC 3&#8242;</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">Gly172Ala</td></tr></tbody></table><table-wrap-foot><fn><p textid="26">The mismatched nt in each mutagenic primer is shown in bold.</p></fn></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap>
The megaprimer PCR method for in vitro mutagenesis (43) was used to introduce the rne-172, rne-186 or rne-187 point mutations into plasmids pUGK19 (rne+), pUGK20 (rne-1) or pUGK46 (rne-3071). The PCR products were subsequently cleaved with EcoRI and SphI and cloned into pUGK19, pUGK20 or pUGK46, respectively, which had been linearized with the same enzymes to generate pHK6 (rne-1/172), pUGK36 (rne-187), pUGK43 (rne-186), pUGK34 (rne-1/187), pUGK 42 (rne-1/186) and pUGK47 (rne-3071/187). The resulting plasmids were then transformed into SK6610 (rne-1 recA56). Subsequently, these plasmids were also transformed into SK9714 [rneDelta1018/pSBK1 (rne+ Cmr)] and the resident pSBK1 (rne+ Cmr) plasmid was displaced to generate the strains used in this study (Table 1). The nucleotide sequences of the mutagenic primers used to generate point mutations are shown in Table 2. Table 2.Mutagenic primers used in this studyPrimerSequenceAmino acid changephk1-mut5' GTGTTCAGAAGGAAGCTTAAATCC 3'Arg187Leuphk5-mut5' GTTTCAGACGGCAGCTTAAATCCC 3'Phe186CysPhk6-mut5' ATTTGCCGACGGCAGCGGTGCGCAC 3'Gly172AlaThe mismatched nt in each mutagenic primer is shown in bold.
###end p 24
###begin p 25
Mutagenic primers used in this study
###end p 25
###begin p 26
The mismatched nt in each mutagenic primer is shown in bold.
###end p 26
###begin p 27
###xml 51 54 51 54 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 181 184 181 184 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 280 281 280 281 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 381 393 381 389 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 404 405 400 401 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 401 405 397 401 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 408 409 404 405 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 483 484 479 480 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
To construct single-copy plasmids carrying various rne alleles used in this study, the multiple-copy plasmids, pHK2, pHK5, pHK6 and pUGK36 were cleaved with EcoRI and NotI and each rne-containing fragment was cloned into pMOK40 that had also been digested with these two enzymes (8). The resulting plasmids, pHK4, pHK7, pHK8 and pUGK44, respectively, were transformed into SK9714 [rneDelta1018/pSBK1 (rne+ Cmr)]. The resident pSBK1 plasmid was subsequently displaced with pWSK129 (Kmr), based on plasmid incompatibility, to generate the strains used in this study.
###end p 27
###begin title 28
RNA preparation and northern analysis
###end title 28
###begin p 29
###xml 101 107 101 107 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al.</italic>
###xml 109 111 109 111 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B44">44</xref>
###xml 126 128 126 128 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B45">45</xref>
###xml 242 247 240 245 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 582 583 574 575 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">8</sup>
###xml 641 643 627 629 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B46">46</xref>
###xml 660 661 646 647 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 756 758 738 740 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 894 896 876 878 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
Total RNA was extracted and northern blot analysis carried out according to the procedures of O'Hara et al. (44) and Burnett (45). To visualize tRNA and 9S rRNA processing, 5.0 mug of total RNA from each sample (except for 7.5-10 mug for the rne-1 strains in the tRNA northerns) were electrophoresed in 8% polyacrylamide/7 M urea gels. For mRNA half-life determinations, either 6% polyacrylamide/7 M urea or 1.2% agarose/glyoxal gels were used. For assays of tRNA maturation, total RNA was extracted from cultures that had been shifted from 37degreesC (at a cell density of 1.0 x 108 cells/ml) to 44degreesC for 2 h. Probes for the 5S rRNA (46) and the tRNAs (8) were oligonucleotides complementary to the mature species and were 5'-end-labeled with gamma-32P-ATP and T4 polynucleotide kinase (New England Biolabs, Ipswich, MA, USA). The DNA probes used for the mRNA half-life experiments were 32P-labeled using random primers as specified by the supplier (Ambion, Austin, TX, USA). Northern blots were scanned with a PhosphorImager scanner (Storm 840, GE Healthcare, Piscataway, NJ, USA) and quantified using ImageQuant 5.2 software. Half-lives were determined by linear regression analysis.
###end p 29
###begin title 30
Western blot analysis
###end title 30
###begin p 31
###xml 67 73 67 73 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al.</italic>
###xml 75 76 75 76 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 553 554 534 535 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 443 449 <span type="species:ncbi:9986">rabbit</span>
Western blot analysis was performed essentially as described in Ow et al. (3). Cell lysates were prepared from cultures growing exponentially at 37degreesC or shifted at mid-log from 37degreesC to 44degreesC for 1 h. Total protein (10 mug/lane) was electrophoresed in 6% SDS-PAGE gels and electrotransferred onto Immobilon-P membranes (Millipore). The RNase E protein was detected using ECL Plus western analysis system (GE Healthcare) with a rabbit anti-RNase E MAP antibody (1:2000 dilution) raised against the first 20 amino acids of the N-terminus (3).
###end p 31
###begin title 32
Computer modeling of the suppressor mutations
###end title 32
###begin p 33
###xml 75 81 75 81 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al.</italic>
###xml 83 85 83 85 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 263 265 263 265 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B47">47</xref>
Three PDB entries (2bx2, 2c4r and 2c0b) derived from the work of Callaghan et al. (23) with coordinate information for the catalytic domain of RNase E were selected as homologous templates for molecular modeling using Geno3D (), an online homology modeling tool (47). For the altered RNase E proteins, the inter/intra molecular (dihedral angle and distance) restraints ratios were set to 0.9. The margins in distance restraints and angle restraints were set at 0.5 A and 1.0degrees, respectively. The maximum number of distance restraints was 20 000. Three models were generated and the model with the lowest free energy was used in this work. In this model, 0.4% of the residues occupied disallowed regions of the Ramachandran plot. The root mean square deviation (RMSD) between this model and the templates ranged from 1.46 to 1.57 A. The lowest free-energy model for the altered RNase E proteins and the 3D structure of wild-type RNase E were analyzed and viewed with PyMol ().
###end p 33
###begin title 34
RESULTS
###end title 34
###begin title 35
###xml 44 49 44 49 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
Isolation of second-site suppressors of the rne-1 allele
###end title 35
###begin p 36
###xml 107 114 107 114 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 116 118 116 118 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 168 170 168 170 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 187 199 187 199 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 recA56</italic>
###xml 213 218 213 218 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 306 307 306 307 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 303 307 303 307 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 340 341 340 341 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 337 341 337 341 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 509 514 503 508 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 595 607 589 597 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 671 672 661 662 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 668 672 658 662 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 674 675 664 665 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 736 737 726 727 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 733 737 723 727 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 107 114 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
In previous experiments, we attempted to determine if overexpression of RNase G [a homologue of RNase E in E. coli (32)] could complement the loss of RNase E activity (36). Since SK6610 (rne-1 recA56) and SK5665 (rne-1) are both temperature sensitive for growth, we transformed them with either pUGK24 (rng+, 6-8 copies/cell) or pUGK31 (rng+, 30-50 copies/cell) and tested for the ability of transformants to grow at 44degreesC. When neither plasmid complemented the conditional lethality associated with the rne-1 allele, we tried a different approach. We transformed a strain that carries the rneDelta1018 deletion allele (SK9714) and a pSC101 derivative containing rne+ (3) with pUGK24 (pSC101) and tried to displace the resident rne+ plasmid, based on the incompatibility of the two plasmids.
###end p 36
###begin p 37
###xml 143 148 143 148 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 232 233 214 215 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">8</sup>
###xml 271 276 253 258 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 288 289 270 271 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 285 289 267 271 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 301 306 283 288 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 307 314 289 296 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng:cat</italic>
###xml 326 327 308 309 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 323 327 305 309 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 342 347 324 329 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 356 360 338 342 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng+</italic>
###xml 467 469 443 445 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;8</sup>
###xml 472 474 448 450 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 311 314 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
Although neither approach led to complementation, we hypothesized that it might be possible to obtain spontaneously arising suppressors of the rne-1 allele that would allow cells to grow at 44degreesC. When we spread approximately108 cells/plate from cultures of SK2585 [rne-1/pUGK24 (rng+)], SK2593 [rne-1/rng:cat pUGK31 (rng+)] and SK2594 [rne-1/pUGK31 (rng+)] at 44degreesC, we obtained a small number of colonies that grew after 24-48 h (frequency of 0.3-7.0 x 10-8) (36). When purified and retested, these isolates grew at 44degreesC ('Materials and methods' section). We subsequently chose four independently identified isolates for further analysis.
###end p 37
###begin p 38
###xml 122 127 122 127 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 160 163 160 163 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 264 265 264 265 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 267 269 267 269 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B41">41</xref>
###xml 440 445 440 445 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 464 476 464 476 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 recA56</italic>
###xml 517 520 511 514 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 717 725 711 719 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 930 938 924 932 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 984 989 978 983 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
Since we hypothesized that at least some of the temperature resistant revertants arose from intragenic suppression of the rne-1 allele, we amplified the entire rne gene from four isolates using PCR and cloned them into pWSK129, a low-copy number vector carrying Kmr (41) (See 'Materials and methods' section) to generate plasmids pHK1, pHK2, pHK5 and pHK6. After determining via replica plating that each of these plasmids complemented the rne-1 allele in SK6610 (rne-1 recA56) at 44degreesC, we sequenced the entire rne regulatory and coding region from each plasmid. To our surprise, plasmids pHK1 and pHK2 each contained a G to T single nucleotide change resulting in an Arg to Leu substitution at amino acid 187 (Figure 1). Plasmid pHK5 carried a T to G nucleotide change causing a Phe to Cys substitution at amino acid 186, while pHK6 contained a G to C mutation that resulted in a Gly to Ala substitution at amino acid 172 (Figure 1). All four clones also retained the original rne-1 mutation.
###end p 38
###begin p 39
###xml 164 165 164 165 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 161 165 161 165 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rng<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 213 225 213 225 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 recA56</italic>
###xml 242 243 242 243 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 239 243 239 243 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 459 464 459 464 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
In order to establish that the isolation of these apparent intragenic second-site suppressor alleles was not dependent on the presence of a plasmid carrying the rng+ gene, we repeated the experiment using SK6610 (rne-1 recA56). The entire rne+ gene and promoter region was cloned and sequenced from seven independently isolated temperature resistant revertants as described above. From these seven independent isolates we identified one true revertant of the rne-1 allele as well as the same three suppressor alleles described above (data not shown).
###end p 39
###begin p 40
###xml 13 20 13 20 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 128 129 124 125 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 112 129 112 125 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018/rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 308 309 304 305 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 305 309 301 305 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 343 365 339 357 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018/rne-1/187</italic>
###xml 376 398 368 386 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018/rne-1/186</italic>
###xml 412 434 400 418 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018/rne-1/172</italic>
###xml 485 488 469 472 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne</italic>
###xml 635 636 619 620 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 656 665 640 649 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/187</italic>
###xml 674 683 658 667 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/186</italic>
###xml 695 704 679 688 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/172</italic>
###xml 784 796 768 776 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
For detailed in vivo analysis of the double mutants, plasmids pHK2, pHK5 and pHK6 were transformed into SK9714 (rneDelta1018/rne+) to determine if they could support cell viability in the complete absence of RNase E activity. All three plasmids displaced the resident plasmid pSBK1 carrying the wild-type rne+ gene, generating strains SK2742 (rneDelta1018/rne-1/187), SK3221 (rneDelta1018/rne-1/186) and SK3222 (rneDelta1018/rne-1/172). Subsequently, the inserts containing the entire rne gene along with its promoter sequences were transferred from pHK2, pHK5 and pHK6 (6-8 copies/cell) and cloned into a single copy vector (pMOK40) (8) to generate pHK4 (rne-1/187), pHK7 (rne-1/186) and pHK8 (rne-1/172), respectively. These plasmids were also able to support cell viability in the rneDelta1018 deletion strain at both 37 and 44degreesC.
###end p 40
###begin p 41
###xml 19 26 19 26 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-172</italic>
###xml 28 35 28 35 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-186</italic>
###xml 40 47 40 47 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-187</italic>
###xml 140 145 140 145 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 221 226 221 226 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 319 331 319 331 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1 recA56</italic>
###xml 401 421 401 421 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/187, rne-1/186</italic>
###xml 426 435 426 435 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/172</italic>
To verify that the rne-172, rne-186 and rne-187 alleles were solely responsible for suppression of the growth phenotype associated with the rne-1 mutation, we introduced the same point mutations individually into pUGK20 (rne-1) using site-directed mutagenesis. The resulting plasmids were then transformed into SK6610 (rne-1 recA56). The plasmids generated by site-directed mutagenesis containing the rne-1/187, rne-1/186 and rne-1/172 double mutations all supported cell viability at 44degreesC (data not shown).
###end p 41
###begin title 42
###xml 44 53 44 53 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/172</italic>
###xml 55 64 55 64 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/186</italic>
###xml 69 78 69 78 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1/187</italic>
Growth properties of strains containing the rne-1/172, rne-1/186 and rne-1/187 double mutations
###end title 42

###xml 2155 2156 2115 2116 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">a</sup>
###xml 2155 2192 2115 2146 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="47"><sup>a</sup>Temperature sensitive at 44&#176;C.</p>
###xml 2155 2192 2115 2146 <fn xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="TF1"><p textid="47"><sup>a</sup>Temperature sensitive at 44&#176;C.</p></fn>
###xml 2015 2192 1981 2146 <table-wrap-foot xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><fn><p textid="46">Cultures were grown as described in Materials and methods section and shifted to 44&#176;C for the determination of their generation times.</p></fn><fn id="TF1"><p textid="47"><sup>a</sup>Temperature sensitive at 44&#176;C.</p></fn></table-wrap-foot>
###xml 1860 2192 1826 2146 <table-wrap xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="T3" position="float"><label>Table 3.</label><caption><p textid="45">Generation times (min) of strains carrying various <italic>rne</italic> alleles</p></caption><table frame="hsides" rules="groups"><thead align="left"><tr><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Allele</th><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">6&#8211;8 copies</th><th rowspan="1" colspan="1">Single copy</th></tr></thead><tbody align="left"><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>rne<sup>+</sup></italic></td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">31</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">32</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>rne-1</italic></td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">TS<xref ref-type="table-fn" rid="TF1"><sup>a</sup></xref></td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">TS<xref ref-type="table-fn" rid="TF1"><sup>a</sup></xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>rne-1/172</italic></td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">36</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">47</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>rne-1/186</italic></td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">45</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">68</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>rne-1/187</italic></td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">35</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1">33</td></tr></tbody></table><table-wrap-foot><fn><p textid="46">Cultures were grown as described in Materials and methods section and shifted to 44&#176;C for the determination of their generation times.</p></fn><fn id="TF1"><p textid="47"><sup>a</sup>Temperature sensitive at 44&#176;C.</p></fn></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap>
In order to establish how well the rne-172, rne-186 and rne-187 alleles suppressed the growth deficiency of an rne-1 mutant at 44degreesC when present in 6-8 copies/cell, we grew cultures of SK9714 (rne+), SK3222 (rne-1/172), SK3221 (rne-1/186), SK2742 (rne-1/187) and SK9937 (rne-1) at 30degreesC in Luria broth and then shifted the exponentially growing cultures to 44degreesC. While the rne-1 mutant stopped growing within 2 h after shift to 44degreesC as previously observed (3), the three strains containing the different double mutants continued to grow, albeit with slightly longer generation times than the wild-type control (Figure 2A, Table 3). SK3221 (rne-1/186) consistently grew slower than either SK3222 (rne-1/172) or SK2742 (rne-1/187) (Table 3). When we performed a similar experiment with the double mutations present on single copy plasmids, the generation time of the rne-1/186 double mutant (SK3223) was 68 min at 44degreesC compared with 32 min for the rne+ control (SK10143, Figure 2B, Table 3). In contrast, the rne-1/172 (SK3224) strain was intermediate in growth, while the rne-1/187 (SK3208) isolate grew at a rate almost identical to the wild-type control strain (SK10143) (Table 3). Figure 2.Growth curves of rneDelta1018 strains carrying various rne alleles. Cultures were grown in Luria broth as described in 'Materials and methods' section. (A) 6-8 copies of various rne alleles. Filled triangle, rne+ (SK9714); Open circle, rne-1/172 (SK3222); filled square, rne-1/187 (SK2742); filled diamond, rne-1/186 (SK3221); open square, rne-1 (SK9937); open triangle, rne-3071 (SK3742); open diamond, rne-3071/187 (SK3743). (B) single copies of the various rne alleles. Filled triangle, rne+ (SK10143); open circle, rne-1/172 (SK3224); filled square, rne-1/187 (SK3208); filled diamond, rne-1/186 (SK3223); open square, rne-1 (SK10144).Table 3.Generation times (min) of strains carrying various rne allelesAllele6-8 copiesSingle copyrne+3132rne-1TSaTSarne-1/1723647rne-1/1864568rne-1/1873533Cultures were grown as described in Materials and methods section and shifted to 44degreesC for the determination of their generation times.aTemperature sensitive at 44degreesC.
###end p 43

###begin p 48
###xml 110 117 110 117 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-186</italic>
###xml 121 128 121 128 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-187</italic>
###xml 275 287 275 283 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 291 292 287 288 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 288 292 284 288 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 512 513 496 497 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 509 513 493 497 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 552 559 536 543 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-186</italic>
###xml 564 571 548 555 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-187</italic>
###xml 647 654 631 638 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-172</italic>
We used site-directed mutagenesis (see 'Materials and methods' section) to construct plasmids carrying either rne-186 or rne-187 single mutants. Somewhat surprisingly, the growth of strains carrying these alleles in multiple copies was indistinguishable from that of SK9714 (rneDelta1018/rne+) at 44degreesC (data not shown). In addition, the growth rates at 37degreesC of strains carrying single copies of the individual suppressor alleles were also comparable to those of the corresponding strains carrying rne+ (data not shown), suggesting that the rne-186 and rne-187 alleles did not significantly affect RNase E activity. We did not test the rne-172 allele.
###end p 48
###begin title 49
The suppressor alleles do not restore autoregulation of RNase E at 44degreesC
###end title 49

###xml 2155 2156 2111 2112 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 2227 2239 2181 2189 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne&#916;1018</italic>
###xml 1597 2359 1561 2309 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="51">Western blot analysis of RNase E levels in various strains. Total protein was isolated from each of the strains after they had been shifted to 44&#176;C for 60 min as described in &#8216;Materials and methods&#8217; section. RNase E was detected with a MAP antibody directed against the first 20 amino acids of protein (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>) and was detected using the ECL Plus system (GE Healthcare). Quantitation was obtained using a Storm 840 PhosphorImager (GE Healthcare). The relative amount of RNase E in each strain as compared to the <italic>rne<sup>+</sup></italic> control represents the average &#177; SEM (<italic>n</italic> &#8805; 3). All alleles were in single copy/cell in a strain carrying the <italic>rne&#916;1018</italic> deletion allele in the chromosome. Lane 1, SK10143; Lane 2, SK10144; Lane 3, SK3208; Lane 4, SK3224 and Lane 5, SK3223.</p>
###xml 1597 2359 1561 2309 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="51">Western blot analysis of RNase E levels in various strains. ###xml 2359 2359 2309 2309 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkn476f3"/>
###xml 1588 2359 1552 2309 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F3" position="float"><label>Figure 3.</label><caption><p textid="51">Western blot analysis of RNase E levels in various strains. Total protein was isolated from each of the strains after they had been shifted to 44&#176;C for 60 min as described in &#8216;Materials and methods&#8217; section. RNase E was detected with a MAP antibody directed against the first 20 amino acids of protein (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>) and was detected using the ECL Plus system (GE Healthcare). Quantitation was obtained using a Storm 840 PhosphorImager (GE Healthcare). The relative amount of RNase E in each strain as compared to the <italic>rne<sup>+</sup></italic> control represents the average &#177; SEM (<italic>n</italic> &#8805; 3). All alleles were in single copy/cell in a strain carrying the <italic>rne&#916;1018</italic> deletion allele in the chromosome. Lane 1, SK10143; Lane 2, SK10144; Lane 3, SK3208; Lane 4, SK3224 and Lane 5, SK3223.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkn476f3"/></fig>
Since RNase E autoregulates its own synthesis via a feedback loop that involves controlling the rate of degradation of its mRNA, alterations in RNase E activity caused by mutations such as rne-1 lead to increased protein levels at both 37 and 44degreesC (3,21). Accordingly, we tested if autoregulation of RNase E levels at 44degreesC was restored by the intragenic second-site suppressor alleles. Quantitative western blot analysis of RNase E protein levels at both 44 and 37degreesC was performed in strains carrying the rne+, rne-1, rne-1/187, rne-1/186 and rne-1/172 alleles in 6-8 copies (data not shown) or in single copy/cell (Figure 3). At 44degreesC there was a 6.9-fold increase in the amount of the full-length protein in the rne-1 strain compared to rne+ strain (Figure 3, lanes 1 and 2). Strikingly, the RNase E levels in all three double mutants were similar to those observed in the rne-1 single mutant (Figure 3, lanes 2-5). In contrast, the protein level in the rne-187 and rne-186 single mutants were experimentally equivalent to what was observed in the wild-type strain, suggesting that autoregulation was unaffected in either single mutant (data not shown). When we carried out an identical experiment at 37degreesC, the levels of the RNase E protein were equivalent for all the strains except rne-1, suggesting that autoregulation was restored by the intragenic second-site suppressors at 37degreesC (data not shown). In the case of rne-1 strain there was a small, but reproducible increase in the amount of both full-length and breakdown products (data not shown). Figure 3.Western blot analysis of RNase E levels in various strains. Total protein was isolated from each of the strains after they had been shifted to 44degreesC for 60 min as described in 'Materials and methods' section. RNase E was detected with a MAP antibody directed against the first 20 amino acids of protein (3) and was detected using the ECL Plus system (GE Healthcare). Quantitation was obtained using a Storm 840 PhosphorImager (GE Healthcare). The relative amount of RNase E in each strain as compared to the rne+ control represents the average +/- SEM (n >/= 3). All alleles were in single copy/cell in a strain carrying the rneDelta1018 deletion allele in the chromosome. Lane 1, SK10143; Lane 2, SK10144; Lane 3, SK3208; Lane 4, SK3224 and Lane 5, SK3223.
###end p 50

RNase E plays a central role in the endonucleolytic initiation of decay of a wide variety of cellular mRNAs (3,5), a role that has historically been viewed as its quintessential in vivo function (48). The failure of the suppressor mutations to restore normal autoregulation of RNase E protein levels at 44degreesC (Figure 3) suggested that the altered enzymes were probably still defective in their ability to initiate the endonucleolytic decay of specific mRNAs. To test this hypothesis directly, we measured the half-lives of three known RNase E-dependent transcripts, cpsE, rpsT and rpsO (49-51) at 44degreesC in strains carrying single copies of the rne+, rne-1, rne-1/187, rne-1/186 and rne-1/172 alleles. In agreement with previously reported results (3,36), the half-life of the major rpsOP1-t1 transcript (52) increased significantly from 2.8 min in the rne+ control to 10.9 min in the rne-1 single mutant (Table 4). Surprisingly, the half-life of this transcript increased further in the rne-1/187 double mutant to 16.3 min. In contrast, the half-life of the rpsOP1-RIII transcript was almost the same in the two mutant strains and was higher than in the rne+ control. The half-lives of the cspE and rpsT mRNAs in the rne-1/187 double mutant were also similar to the rne-1 single mutant (Table 4) and were 2-4-fold longer than in the rne+ control. In the rne-1/186 and rne-1/172 double mutants there was a marginal reduction in the half-lives of the rpsT and cspE transcripts relative to the rne-1 single mutant (Table 4), but the half-lives were still 2- to 3-fold longer than in the rne+ control. Although the mRNA half-life data directly correlated with the failure of the suppressor alleles to restore normal autoregulation of RNase E protein levels in all three double mutant strains (Figure 3), it did not correlate with the generation times reported in Table 3. ###end p 53

###begin title 57
5S rRNA processing is restored to wild-type levels in the double mutants

###begin p 58
###xml 113 114 113 114 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B6">6</xref>
###xml 175 180 175 180 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rne-1</italic>
###xml 222 223 222 223 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 224 225 224 225 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B6">6</xref>
###xml 440 441 440 441 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>

RNase E was initially discovered based on its role in the maturation of 5S rRNA from a larger 9S rRNA precursor (6), such that unprocessed 5S rRNA precursors accumulate in an rne-1 mutant at the nonpermissive temperature (3,6). To determine if 5S rRNA precursors were processed with increased efficiency in the presence of the intragenic second-site suppressor mutations, we prepared total RNA from strains carrying single copies of the rne+, rne-1, rne-1/172, rne-1/186 and rne-1/187 alleles after growth at 44degreesC for 2 h and used northern analysis to measure the processed fraction (PF) for 5S rRNA. As shown in Figure 4, the Processed Fraction (PF) values of mature 5S rRNA in all three double mutants were essentially identical to that observed in the rne+ control. Figure 4.Analysis of 9S rRNA processing in various strains. Total steady-state RNA was isolated from the various strains after the cultures had been shifted to 44degreesC for 120 min as described in 'Materials and methods' section. Five micrograms of total RNA was loaded in each lane of an 8% polyacrylamide/7 M urea gel. The northern blot was subsequently probed with an oligonucleotide specific for the mature 5S rRNA (55). PF (processed fraction) denotes the fraction of the mature 5S rRNA relative to the total amount of both processed and unprocessed species in each specific genetic background. All alleles were in single copy/cell in a strain carrying the rneDelta1018 deletion. The data shown represents the average +/- SEM (n >/= 3). Lane 1, SK10143; Lane 2, SK10144; Lane 3, SK3208; Lane 4, SK3224 and Lane 5, SK3223.

###xml 1409 2422 1401 2398 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F5" position="float"><label>Figure 5.</label><caption><p textid="62">Analysis of tRNA maturation in various strains. Lane 1, SK10143; Lane 2, SK10144; Lane 3, SK3208; Lane 4, SK3224 and Lane 5, SK3223.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkn476f5"/></fig>
RNase E is required for catalyzing the conversion of some polycistronic tRNA transcripts into pre-tRNAs that undergo further processing by RNase P and various 3' --> 5' exonucleases (7,8). In fact, it has been hypothesized that RNase E is essential for the cell because of its role in tRNA maturation (8). To determine if the altered RNase E proteins were able to process precursor tRNAs, we examined the maturation of tRNAPro, tRNAHis and tRNACys in strains carrying single copies of the various rne alleles. We chose these tRNAs because their maturation has been shown to be highly dependent on RNase E, and there is significant accumulation of their precursor species in an rne-1 strain (8). Total RNA, from rne+, rne-1, rne-1/172, rne-1/186 and rne-1/187 strains was obtained after shifting exponentially growing cultures to 44degreesC for 2 h. As expected, northern blot analysis demonstrated that the maturation of each tRNA species was significantly impaired in the rne-1 mutant compared to the wild-type control (Figure 5A and B and data not shown). For example, the PF values of tRNACys and tRNAHis decreased 2.6- and 4.3-fold, respectively, in the rne-1 strain (Figure 5A and B). In contrast, all three intragenic second-site suppressor alleles led to an almost complete restoration of rne+ tRNA maturation levels as measured by the PF value of each transcript (Figure 5A and B and data not shown). Figure 5.Analysis of tRNA maturation in various strains. Total steady-state RNA was isolated from various strains after the cultures had been shifted to 44degreesC for 120 min as described in 'Materials and methods' section. Typically 5 mug of total RNA was loaded in each lane (except for the rne-1 mutants where 7.5 mug of RNA were loaded for better visualization of mature species) of an 8% polyacrylamide/7 M urea gel. The northern blot was subsequently sequentially probed with oligonucleotides (8) specific for either (A) tRNACys, (B) tRNAHis or tRNAPro (data not shown). PF (processed fraction) denotes the fraction of the mature tRNA relative to the total amount of both processed and unprocessed species in each genetic background. All alleles were in single copy/cell in an rneDelta1018 deletion strain. The PF values are the average +/- SEM (n >/= 3). Leftward arrow indicates the position of the mature tRNA species. Lane 1, SK10143; Lane 2, SK10144; Lane 3, SK3208; Lane 4, SK3224 and Lane 5, SK3223.
###end p 61
